<p>The <b>Enlightenment</b> (also known as the <b>Age of Enlightenment</b> or the <b>Age of Reason</b>;<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr"><i>le Siècle des Lumières</i></span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a>&#160;</small>'"the Century of Lights"'; and in <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de">Aufklärung</span>, "Enlightenment")<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> was an intellectual and philosophical movement which dominated the world of ideas in Europe during the 18th century, "The Century of Philosophy".<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Enlightenment included a range of ideas centered on reason as the primary source of authority and legitimacy<i>—</i>and came to advance ideals like <a href="/wiki/Liberty" title="Liberty">liberty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Idea_of_progress" title="Idea of progress">progress</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toleration" title="Toleration">tolerance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fraternity_(philosophy)" title="Fraternity (philosophy)">fraternity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_government" class="mw-redirect" title="Constitutional government">constitutional government</a> and <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">separation of church and state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> In France the central doctrines of the Enlightenment philosophers were <a href="/wiki/Individual_liberty" class="mw-redirect" title="Individual liberty">individual liberty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Religious_tolerance" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious tolerance">religious tolerance</a>, in opposition to an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolute monarchy</a> and the fixed dogmas of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>. The Enlightenment was marked by an emphasis on the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reductionism" title="Reductionism">reductionism</a> along with increased questioning of religious orthodoxy—an attitude captured by the phrase <i><a href="/wiki/Sapere_aude" title="Sapere aude">Sapere aude</a></i>, "Dare to know".<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>French historians traditionally place the Enlightenment between 1715 (the year that <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis XIV">Louis XIV</a> died) and 1789 (the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>). Some recent historians begin the period in the 1620s, with the start of the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_revolution" title="Scientific revolution">scientific revolution</a>. <i><span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">Les philosophes</span></i> (French&#160;for <span lang="" xml:lang="">"the philosophers"</span>) of the period widely circulated their ideas through meetings at scientific academies, <a href="/wiki/Masonic_lodge" title="Masonic lodge">Masonic lodges</a>, literary salons, coffee houses and printed books and pamphlets. The ideas of the Enlightenment undermined the authority of the monarchy and the Church—and paved the way for the political revolutions of the 18th and 19th centuries. A variety of 19th-century movements, including <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neo-classicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-classicism">neo-classicism</a>, trace their intellectual heritage back to the Enlightenment.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Age of Enlightenment was preceded by and closely associated with the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_revolution" title="Scientific revolution">scientific revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Earlier philosophers whose work influenced the Enlightenment included <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The major figures of the Enlightenment included <a href="/wiki/Cesare_Beccaria" title="Cesare Beccaria">Cesare Beccaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denis_Diderot" title="Denis Diderot">Denis Diderot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a>. Some European rulers, including <a href="/wiki/Catherine_II_of_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Catherine II of Russia">Catherine II of Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_II_of_Austria" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph II of Austria">Joseph II of Austria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_II_of_Prussia" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick II of Prussia">Frederick II of Prussia</a>, tried to apply Enlightenment thought on religious and political tolerance, which became known as <a href="/wiki/Enlightened_absolutism" title="Enlightened absolutism">enlightened absolutism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a> visited Europe repeatedly and contributed actively to the scientific and political debates there and brought the newest ideas back to Philadelphia. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> closely followed European ideas and later incorporated some of the ideals of the Enlightenment into the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence (1776)</a>. One of his peers, <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>, incorporated these ideals into the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a> during its framing in 1787.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most influential publication of the Enlightenment was the <i><span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr"><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A9die" title="Encyclopédie">Encyclopédie</a></span></i> (<i>Encyclopaedia</i>). Published between 1751 and 1772 in thirty-five volumes, it was compiled by Denis Diderot, <a href="/wiki/Jean_le_Rond_d%27Alembert" title="Jean le Rond d'Alembert">Jean le Rond d'Alembert</a> (until 1759) and a team of 150 scientists and philosophers. It helped spread the ideas of the Enlightenment across Europe and beyond.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Other landmark publications were Voltaire's <i><a href="/wiki/Dictionnaire_philosophique" title="Dictionnaire philosophique">Dictionnaire philosophique</a></i> (<i>Philosophical Dictionary</i>; 1764) and <i><a href="/wiki/Letters_on_the_English" title="Letters on the English">Letters on the English</a></i> (1733); Rousseau's <i><a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_Inequality" title="Discourse on Inequality">Discourse on Inequality</a></i> (1754) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Social_Contract" title="The Social Contract">The Social Contract</a></i> (1762); Adam Smith's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wealth_of_Nations" title="The Wealth of Nations">The Wealth of Nations</a></i> (1776); and <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Spirit_of_the_Laws" title="The Spirit of the Laws">The Spirit of the Laws</a></i> (1748). The ideas of the Enlightenment played a major role in inspiring the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, which began in 1789. After the Revolution, the Enlightenment was followed by the intellectual movement known as <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romanticism</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Enlightenment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>' <a href="/wiki/Rationalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Rationalist">rationalist</a> philosophy laid the foundation for enlightenment thinking. His attempt to construct the sciences on a secure metaphysical foundation was not as successful as his <a href="/wiki/Cartesian_doubt" title="Cartesian doubt">method of doubt</a> applied in philosophic areas leading to a <a href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">dualistic doctrine</a> of mind and matter. His skepticism was refined by <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Essay_Concerning_Human_Understanding" class="mw-redirect" title="Essay Concerning Human Understanding">Essay Concerning Human Understanding</a> (1690) and <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>'s writings in the 1740s. His dualism was challenged by <a href="/wiki/Spinoza" class="mw-redirect" title="Spinoza">Spinoza</a>'s uncompromising assertion of the unity of matter in his <a href="/wiki/Tractatus_Theologico-Politicus" title="Tractatus Theologico-Politicus">Tractatus</a> (1670) and <a href="/wiki/Ethics_(Spinoza)" title="Ethics (Spinoza)">Ethics</a> (1677).</p>
<p>These laid down two distinct lines of Enlightenment thought: the moderate variety, following Descartes, Locke and <a href="/wiki/Christian_Wolff_(philosopher)" title="Christian Wolff (philosopher)">Christian Wolff</a> which sought accommodation between reform and the traditional systems of power and faith and the radical enlightenment, inspired by the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Spinoza" class="mw-redirect" title="Spinoza">Spinoza</a>, advocating democracy, individual liberty, freedom of expression and eradication of religious authority<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsrael200615_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsrael200615-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsrael2010vii.E2.80.93viii.2C_19_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsrael2010vii.E2.80.93viii.2C_19-14">[14]</a></sup> The moderate variety tended to be <a href="/wiki/Deistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Deistic">deistic</a> whereas the radical tendency separated the basis of morality entirely from theology. Both lines of thought were eventually opposed by a conservative <a href="/wiki/Counter-Enlightenment" title="Counter-Enlightenment">Counter-Enlightenment</a>, which sought a return to faith.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsrael201011_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsrael201011-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the mid-18th century, Paris became the center of an explosion of philosophic and scientific activity challenging traditional doctrines and dogmas. The philosophic movement was led by <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a>, who argued for a society based upon reason rather than faith and Catholic doctrine, for a new civil order based on natural law and for science based on experiments and observation. The political philosopher <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a> introduced the idea of a <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers" title="Separation of powers">separation of powers</a> in a government, a concept which was enthusiastically adopted by the authors of the United States Constitution. While the <i>Philosophes</i> of the French Enlightenment were not revolutionaries and many were members of the nobility, their ideas played an important part in undermining the legitimacy of the Old Regime and shaping the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetitfils200599.E2.80.93105_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetitfils200599.E2.80.93105-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Immanuel_Kant_(painted_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Immanuel_Kant_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg/170px-Immanuel_Kant_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg" width="170" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Immanuel_Kant_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg/255px-Immanuel_Kant_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Immanuel_Kant_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg/340px-Immanuel_Kant_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="964" data-file-height="1388" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Immanuel_Kant_(painted_portrait).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
German philosopher <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Francis_Hutcheson_(philosopher)" title="Francis Hutcheson (philosopher)">Francis Hutcheson</a>, a moral philosopher, described the <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Consequentialism" title="Consequentialism">consequentialist</a> principle that virtue is that which provides, in his words, "the greatest happiness for the greatest numbers". Much of what is incorporated in the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> (the nature of knowledge, evidence, experience and causation) and some modern attitudes towards the relationship between science and religion were developed by his protégés <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Denby_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denby-17">[17]</a></sup> Hume became a major figure in the <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">skeptical philosophical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Empiricist" class="mw-redirect" title="Empiricist">empiricist</a> traditions of philosophy.</p>
<p>Immanuel Kant (1724–1804) tried to reconcile <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a> and religious belief, individual freedom and political authority, as well as map out a view of the public sphere through private and public reason.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Kant's work continued to shape German thought and indeed all of European philosophy, well into the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mary_Wollstonecraft" title="Mary Wollstonecraft">Mary Wollstonecraft</a> was one of England's earliest <a href="/wiki/Feminist" class="mw-redirect" title="Feminist">feminist</a> philosophers.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> She argued for a society based on reason and that women as well as men should be treated as rational beings. She is best known for her work <i><a href="/wiki/A_Vindication_of_the_Rights_of_Woman" title="A Vindication of the Rights of Woman">A Vindication of the Rights of Woman</a></i> (1791).<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science">Science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Enlightenment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Science in the Age of Enlightenment">Science in the Age of Enlightenment</a></div>
<p>Science played an important role in Enlightenment discourse and thought. Many Enlightenment writers and thinkers had backgrounds in the sciences and associated scientific advancement with the overthrow of religion and traditional authority in favour of the development of free speech and thought. Scientific progress during the Enlightenment included the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (fixed air) by the chemist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Black" title="Joseph Black">Joseph Black</a>, the argument for <a href="/wiki/Deep_time" title="Deep time">deep time</a> by the geologist <a href="/wiki/James_Hutton" title="James Hutton">James Hutton</a> and the invention of the steam engine by <a href="/wiki/James_Watt" title="James Watt">James Watt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The experiments of Lavoisier were used to create the first modern chemical plants in Paris and the experiments of the <a href="/wiki/Montgolfier_Brothers" class="mw-redirect" title="Montgolfier Brothers">Montgolfier Brothers</a> enabled them to launch the first manned flight in a hot-air balloon on 21 November 1783 from the <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_la_Muette" title="Château de la Muette">Château de la Muette</a>, near the <a href="/wiki/Bois_de_Boulogne" title="Bois de Boulogne">Bois de Boulogne</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Broadly speaking, Enlightenment science greatly valued <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a> and rational thought and was embedded with the Enlightenment ideal of advancement and progress. The study of science, under the heading of <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a>, was divided into physics and a conglomerate grouping of chemistry and <a href="/wiki/Natural_history" title="Natural history">natural history</a>, which included <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomy</a>, biology, <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mineralogy" title="Mineralogy">mineralogy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> As with most Enlightenment views, the benefits of science were not seen universally: Rousseau criticized the sciences for distancing man from nature and not operating to make people happier.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Science during the Enlightenment was dominated by scientific societies and <a href="/wiki/Academy" title="Academy">academies</a>, which had largely replaced universities as centres of scientific research and development. Societies and academies were also the backbone of the maturation of the scientific profession. Another important development was the <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">popularization</a> of science among an increasingly literate population. <a href="/wiki/Philosophe" class="mw-redirect" title="Philosophe">Philosophes</a> introduced the public to many scientific theories, most notably through the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A9die" title="Encyclopédie">Encyclopédie</a></i> and the popularization of <a href="/wiki/Newtonianism" title="Newtonianism">Newtonianism</a> by <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> and Émilie du Châtelet. Some historians have marked the 18th century as a drab period in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_science" title="History of science">history of science</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> However, the century saw significant advancements in the practice of <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>; the development of biological <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomy</a>; a new understanding of <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">magnetism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>; and the maturation of <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a> as a discipline, which established the foundations of modern chemistry.</p>
<p>Scientific academies and societies grew out of the Scientific Revolution as the creators of scientific knowledge in contrast to the scholasticism of the university.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> During the Enlightenment, some societies created or retained links to universities, but contemporary sources distinguished universities from scientific societies by claiming that the university's utility was in the transmission of knowledge while societies functioned to create knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> As the role of universities in institutionalized science began to diminish, learned societies became the cornerstone of organized science. Official scientific societies were chartered by the state in order to provide technical expertise.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Most societies were granted permission to oversee their own publications, control the election of new members and the administration of the society.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> After 1700, a tremendous number of official academies and societies were founded in Europe and by 1789 there were over seventy official scientific societies. In reference to this growth, <a href="/wiki/Bernard_de_Fontenelle" class="mw-redirect" title="Bernard de Fontenelle">Bernard de Fontenelle</a> coined the term "the Age of Academies" to describe the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>The influence of science also began appearing more commonly in poetry and literature during the Enlightenment. Some poetry became infused with <a href="/wiki/Scientific_metaphor" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific metaphor">scientific metaphor</a> and imagery, while other poems were written directly about scientific topics. <a href="/wiki/Sir_Richard_Blackmore" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Richard Blackmore">Sir Richard Blackmore</a> committed the Newtonian system to verse in <i>Creation, a Philosophical Poem in Seven Books</i> (1712). After Newton's death in 1727, poems were composed in his honour for decades.<sup id="cite_ref-Burns.2C_2003.2C_entry:_158_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burns.2C_2003.2C_entry:_158-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_Thomson_(poet)" class="mw-redirect" title="James Thomson (poet)">James Thomson</a> (1700–1748) penned his "Poem to the Memory of Newton", which mourned the loss of Newton, but also praised his science and legacy.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Sociology,_economics_and_law"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sociology.2C_economics_and_law">Sociology, economics and law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Enlightenment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Sociology, economics and law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cesare_Beccaria_in_Dei_delitti_crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Cesare_Beccaria_in_Dei_delitti_crop.jpg/170px-Cesare_Beccaria_in_Dei_delitti_crop.jpg" width="170" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Cesare_Beccaria_in_Dei_delitti_crop.jpg/255px-Cesare_Beccaria_in_Dei_delitti_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Cesare_Beccaria_in_Dei_delitti_crop.jpg/340px-Cesare_Beccaria_in_Dei_delitti_crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="759" data-file-height="981" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cesare_Beccaria_in_Dei_delitti_crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Cesare_Beccaria" title="Cesare Beccaria">Cesare Beccaria</a>, father of classical criminal theory (1738–1794)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hume and other Scottish Enlightenment thinkers developed a "<a href="/wiki/Science_of_man" title="Science of man">science of man</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-Magnusson_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magnusson-34">[34]</a></sup> which was expressed historically in works by authors including <a href="/wiki/James_Burnett" class="mw-redirect" title="James Burnett">James Burnett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adam_Ferguson" title="Adam Ferguson">Adam Ferguson</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Millar_(philosopher)" title="John Millar (philosopher)">John Millar</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Robertson_(historian)" title="William Robertson (historian)">William Robertson</a>, all of whom merged a scientific study of how humans behaved in ancient and primitive cultures with a strong awareness of the determining forces of <a href="/wiki/Modernity" title="Modernity">modernity</a>. Modern sociology largely originated from this movement<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> and Hume's philosophical concepts that directly influenced James Madison (and thus the U.S. Constitution) and as popularised by <a href="/wiki/Dugald_Stewart" title="Dugald Stewart">Dugald Stewart</a>, would be the basis of <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">classical liberalism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1776, Adam Smith published <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wealth_of_Nations" title="The Wealth of Nations">The Wealth of Nations</a></i>, often considered the first work on modern economics as it had an immediate impact on British economic policy that continues into the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-Fry_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fry-37">[37]</a></sup> It was immediately preceded and influenced by <a href="/wiki/Anne-Robert-Jacques_Turgot,_Baron_de_Laune" class="mw-redirect" title="Anne-Robert-Jacques Turgot, Baron de Laune">Anne-Robert-Jacques Turgot, Baron de Laune</a> drafts of <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reflections_on_the_Formation_and_Distribution_of_Wealth&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Reflections on the Formation and Distribution of Wealth (page does not exist)">Reflections on the Formation and Distribution of Wealth</a></i> (Paris, 1766). Smith acknowledged indebtedness and possibly was the original English translator.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cesare_Beccaria" title="Cesare Beccaria">Cesare Beccaria</a>, a jurist, criminologist, philosopher and politician and one of the great Enlightenment writers, became famous for his masterpiece <i>Of Crimes and Punishments</i> (1764), later translated into 22 languages,<sup id="cite_ref-history-world.org_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-world.org-39">[39]</a></sup> which condemned torture and the death penalty and was a founding work in the field of penology and the Classical School of criminology by promoting criminal justice. Another prominent intellectual was <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Mario_Pagano" title="Francesco Mario Pagano">Francesco Mario Pagano</a>, who wrote important studies such as <i>Saggi Politici</i> (Political Essays, 1783), one of the major works of the Enlightenment in Naples; and <i>Considerazioni sul processo criminale</i> (Considerations on the criminal trial, 1787), which established him as an international authority on criminal law.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Enlightenment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rousseau.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Rousseau.jpg/170px-Rousseau.jpg" width="170" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Rousseau.jpg/255px-Rousseau.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Rousseau.jpg/340px-Rousseau.jpg 2x" data-file-width="348" data-file-height="445" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rousseau.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Like other Enlightenment philosophers, <a href="/wiki/Rousseau" class="mw-redirect" title="Rousseau">Rousseau</a> was critical of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Enlightenment has long been hailed as the foundation of modern Western political and intellectual culture.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The Enlightenment brought political modernization to the West, in terms of introducing democratic values and institutions and the creation of modern, liberal democracies. This thesis has been widely accepted by Anglophone scholars and has been reinforced by the large-scale studies by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Darnton" title="Robert Darnton">Robert Darnton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roy_Porter" title="Roy Porter">Roy Porter</a> and most recently by Jonathan Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theories_of_government">Theories of government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Enlightenment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Theories of government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Struensee_-_miniature_portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Struensee_-_miniature_portrait.jpg/170px-Struensee_-_miniature_portrait.jpg" width="170" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Struensee_-_miniature_portrait.jpg/255px-Struensee_-_miniature_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Struensee_-_miniature_portrait.jpg/340px-Struensee_-_miniature_portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="477" data-file-height="565" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Struensee_-_miniature_portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Denmark's minister <a href="/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Struensee" title="Johann Friedrich Struensee">Johann Struensee</a>, a social reformer, was publicly executed in 1772</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>John Locke, one of the most influential Enlightenment thinkers,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> based his governance philosophy in <a href="/wiki/Social_contract_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Social contract theory">social contract theory</a>, a subject that permeated Enlightenment political thought. The English philosopher <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a> ushered in this new debate with his work <i><a href="/wiki/Leviathan_(book)" title="Leviathan (book)">Leviathan</a></i> in 1651. Hobbes also developed some of the fundamentals of European <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">liberal thought</a>: the right of the individual; the natural equality of all men; the artificial character of the political order (which led to the later distinction between <a href="/wiki/Civil_society" title="Civil society">civil society</a> and the state); the view that all legitimate political power must be "representative" and based on the consent of the people; and a liberal interpretation of law which leaves people free to do whatever the law does not explicitly forbid.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Both Locke and Rousseau developed social contract theories in <i>Two Treatises of Government</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_Inequality" title="Discourse on Inequality">Discourse on Inequality</a></i>, respectively. While quite different works, Locke, Hobbes and Rousseau agreed that a social contract, in which the government's authority lies in the consent of the governed,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> is necessary for man to live in civil society. Locke defines the state of nature as a condition in which humans are rational and follow natural law, in which all men are born equal and with the right to life, liberty and property. However, when one citizen breaks the Law of Nature both the transgressor and the victim enter into a state of war, from which it is virtually impossible to break free. Therefore, Locke said that individuals enter into civil society to protect their natural rights via an "unbiased judge" or common authority, such as courts, to appeal to. Contrastingly, Rousseau's conception relies on the supposition that "civil man" is corrupted, while "natural man" has no want he cannot fulfill himself. Natural man is only taken out of the state of nature when the inequality associated with private property is established.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Rousseau said that people join into civil society via the social contract to achieve unity while preserving individual freedom. This is embodied in the sovereignty of the <a href="/wiki/General_will" title="General will">general will</a>, the moral and collective legislative body constituted by citizens.</p>
<p>Locke is known for his statement that individuals have a right to "Life, Liberty and Property" and his belief that the natural right to property is derived from labor. Tutored by Locke, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Ashley-Cooper,_3rd_Earl_of_Shaftesbury" title="Anthony Ashley-Cooper, 3rd Earl of Shaftesbury">Anthony Ashley-Cooper, 3rd Earl of Shaftesbury</a> wrote in 1706: "There is a mighty Light which spreads its self over the world especially in those two free Nations of England and Holland; on whom the Affairs of Europe now turn".<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Locke's theory of natural rights has influenced many political documents, including the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">United States Declaration of Independence</a> and the French National Constituent Assembly's <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen" title="Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen">Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen</a>.</p>
<p>The <i>philosophes</i> argued that the establishment of a contractual basis of rights would lead to the <a href="/wiki/Market_mechanism" title="Market mechanism">market mechanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>, religious <a href="/wiki/Toleration" title="Toleration">tolerance</a> and the organization of states into self-governing republics through democratic means. In this view, the tendency of the <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophes" title="Philosophes">philosophes</a></i> in particular to apply <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a> to every problem is considered the essential change.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though much of Enlightenment political thought was dominated by social contract theorists, both David Hume and Adam Ferguson criticized this camp. Hume's essay <i>Of the Original Contract</i> argues that governments derived from consent are rarely seen and civil government is grounded in a ruler's habitual authority and force. It is precisely because of the ruler's authority over-and-against the subject, that the subject tacitly consents and Hume says that the subjects would "never imagine that their consent made him sovereign", rather the authority did so.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Similarly, Ferguson did not believe citizens built the state, rather polities grew out of social development. In his 1767 <i><a href="/wiki/An_Essay_on_the_History_of_Civil_Society" title="An Essay on the History of Civil Society">An Essay on the History of Civil Society</a></i>, Ferguson uses the four stages of progress, a theory that was very popular in Scotland at the time, to explain how humans advance from a hunting and gathering society to a commercial and civil society without "signing" a social contract.</p>
<p>Both Rousseau and Locke's social contract theories rest on the presupposition of <a href="/wiki/Natural_and_legal_rights" title="Natural and legal rights">natural rights</a>, which are not a result of law or custom, but are things that all men have in pre-political societies and are therefore universal and inalienable. The most famous natural right formulation comes from John Locke in his <i>Second Treatise</i>, when he introduces the state of nature. For Locke, the law of nature is grounded on mutual security or the idea that one cannot infringe on another's natural rights, as every man is equal and has the same inalienable rights. These natural rights include perfect equality and freedom, as well as the right to preserve life and property. Locke also argued against slavery on the basis that enslaving yourself goes against the law of nature because you cannot surrender your own rights, your freedom is absolute and no one can take it from you. Additionally, Locke argues that one person cannot enslave another because it is morally reprehensible, although he introduces a caveat by saying that enslavement of a lawful captive in time of war would not go against one's natural rights.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Enlightened_absolutism">Enlightened absolutism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Enlightenment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Enlightened absolutism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Enlightened_absolutism" title="Enlightened absolutism">Enlightened absolutism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Retrato_do_Marques_de_Pombal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Retrato_do_Marques_de_Pombal.jpg/250px-Retrato_do_Marques_de_Pombal.jpg" width="250" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Retrato_do_Marques_de_Pombal.jpg/375px-Retrato_do_Marques_de_Pombal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8a/Retrato_do_Marques_de_Pombal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="437" data-file-height="544" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Retrato_do_Marques_de_Pombal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Sebasti%C3%A3o_Jos%C3%A9_de_Carvalho_e_Melo,_1st_Marquis_of_Pombal" title="Sebastião José de Carvalho e Melo, 1st Marquis of Pombal">Marquis of Pombal</a>, as the head of the government of Portugal, implemented sweeping socio-economic reforms (abolished <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Portugal" title="Slavery in Portugal">slavery</a>, significantly weakened the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Inquisition" title="Portuguese Inquisition">Inquisition</a>, created the basis for secular public schools and restructured the tax system), effectively ruling as a powerful, progressive dictator</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The leaders of the Enlightenment were not especially democratic, as they more often look to absolute monarchs as the key to imposing reforms designed by the intellectuals. Voltaire despised democracy and said the absolute monarch must be enlightened and must act as dictated by reason and justice<i>—</i>in other words, be a "philosopher-king".<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>In several nations, rulers welcomed leaders of the Enlightenment at court and asked them to help design laws and programs to reform the system, typically to build stronger states. These rulers are called "enlightened despots" by historians.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> They included <a href="/wiki/Frederick_the_Great" title="Frederick the Great">Frederick the Great</a> of Prussia, <a href="/wiki/Catherine_the_Great" title="Catherine the Great">Catherine the Great</a> of Russia, <a href="/wiki/Leopold_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Leopold II, Holy Roman Emperor">Leopold II</a> of <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Tuscany" title="Grand Duchy of Tuscany">Tuscany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Joseph II, Holy Roman Emperor">Joseph II</a> of Austria. Joseph was over-enthusiastic, announcing many reforms that had little support so that revolts broke out and his regime became a comedy of errors and nearly all his programs were reversed.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Senior ministers <a href="/wiki/Sebasti%C3%A3o_Jos%C3%A9_de_Carvalho_e_Melo,_1st_Marquess_of_Pombal" class="mw-redirect" title="Sebastião José de Carvalho e Melo, 1st Marquess of Pombal">Pombal in Portugal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Struensee" title="Johann Friedrich Struensee">Struensee in Denmark</a> also governed according to Enlightenment ideals. In Poland, the model <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_May_3,_1791" title="Constitution of May 3, 1791">constitution of 1791</a> expressed Enlightenment ideals, but was in effect for only one year before <a href="/wiki/Second_Partition_of_Poland" title="Second Partition of Poland">the nation was partitioned</a> among its neighbors. More enduring were the cultural achievements, which created a nationalist spirit in Poland.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Frederick_the_Great" title="Frederick the Great">Frederick the Great</a>, the king of <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a> from 1740 to 1786, saw himself as a leader of the Enlightenment and patronized philosophers and scientists at his court in Berlin. Voltaire, who had been imprisoned and maltreated by the French government, was eager to accept Frederick's invitation to live at his palace. Frederick explained: "My principal occupation is to combat ignorance and prejudice&#160;... to enlighten minds, cultivate morality, and to make people as happy as it suits human nature, and as the means at my disposal permit".<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_French_Revolution">The French Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Enlightenment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The French Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Enlightenment has been frequently linked to the French Revolution of 1789. One view of the political changes that occurred during the Enlightenment is that the "<a href="/wiki/Consent_of_the_governed" title="Consent of the governed">consent of the governed</a>" philosophy as delineated by Locke in <i><a href="/wiki/Two_Treatises_of_Government" title="Two Treatises of Government">Two Treatises of Government</a></i> (1689) represented a paradigm shift from the old governance paradigm under feudalism known as the "<a href="/wiki/Divine_right_of_kings" title="Divine right of kings">divine right of kings</a>". In this view, the revolutions of the late 1700s and early 1800s were caused by the fact that this governance paradigm shift often could not be resolved peacefully and therefore violent revolution was the result. Clearly a governance philosophy where the king was never wrong was in direct conflict with one whereby citizens by natural law had to consent to the acts and rulings of their government.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alexis_de_Tocqueville" title="Alexis de Tocqueville">Alexis de Tocqueville</a> described the French Revolution as the inevitable result of the radical opposition created in the 18th century between the monarchy and the men of letters of the Enlightenment. These men of letters constituted a sort of "substitute aristocracy that was both all-powerful and without real power". This illusory power came from the rise of "public opinion", born when absolutist centralization removed the nobility and the bourgeoisie from the political sphere. The "literary politics" that resulted promoted a discourse of equality and was hence in fundamental opposition to the monarchical regime.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> De Tocqueville "clearly designates&#160; ... the cultural effects of transformation in the forms of the exercise of power".<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Nevertheless, it took another century before cultural approach became central to the historiography, as typified by Robert Darnton, <i>The Business of Enlightenment: A Publishing History of the Encyclopédie, 1775–1800</i> (1979).</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Israel" title="Jonathan Israel">Jonathan Israel</a> asserts: "The prevailing view about the French Revolution not being caused by books and ideas in the first place may be very widely influential, but it is also, on the basis of the detailed evidence, totally indefensible. Indeed, without referring to Radical Enlightenment nothing about the French Revolution makes the slightest sense or can even begin to be provisionally explained".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsrael2010224_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsrael2010224-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Enlightenment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Enlightenment era religious commentary was a response to the preceding century of religious conflict in Europe, especially the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Theologians of the Enlightenment wanted to reform their faith to its generally non-confrontational roots and to limit the capacity for religious controversy to spill over into politics and warfare while still maintaining a true faith in <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>. For moderate Christians, this meant a return to simple Scripture. John Locke abandoned the corpus of theological commentary in favor of an "unprejudiced examination" of the Word of God alone. He determined the essence of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> to be a belief in <a href="/wiki/Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Christ">Christ</a> the redeemer and recommended avoiding more detailed debate.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> In the <i><a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Bible" title="Jefferson Bible">Jefferson Bible</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> went further and dropped any passages dealing with miracles, visitations of angels and the <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">resurrection of Jesus</a> after <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_of_Jesus" title="Crucifixion of Jesus">his death</a>, as he tried to extract the practical Christian moral code of the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Enlightenment scholars sought to curtail the political power of <a href="/wiki/Organized_religion" title="Organized religion">organized religion</a> and thereby prevent another age of intolerant religious war.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Spinoza" class="mw-redirect" title="Spinoza">Spinoza</a> determined to remove politics from contemporary and historical theology (e.g., disregarding <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">Judaic law</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Moses_Mendelssohn" title="Moses Mendelssohn">Moses Mendelssohn</a> advised affording no political weight to any organized religion, but instead recommended that each person follow what they found most convincing.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> A good religion based in instinctive <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morals</a> and a belief in God should not theoretically need force to maintain order in its believers and both Mendelssohn and Spinoza judged religion on its moral fruits, not the logic of its theology.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>A number of novel ideas about religion developed with the Enlightenment, including <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deism</a> and talk of <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheism</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a>, deism is the simple belief in <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">God the Creator</a>, with no reference to the Bible or any other miraculous source. Instead, the deist relies solely on personal reason to guide his creed,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> which was eminently agreeable to many thinkers of the time.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Atheism was much discussed, but there were few proponents. Wilson and Reill note: "In fact, very few enlightened intellectuals, even when they were vocal critics of Christianity, were true atheists. Rather, they were critics of orthodox belief, wedded rather to skepticism, deism, vitalism, or perhaps pantheism".<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Some followed Pierre Bayle and argued that atheists could indeed be moral men.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Many others like Voltaire held that without belief in a God who punishes evil, the moral order of society was undermined. That is, since atheists gave themselves to no Supreme Authority and no law and had no fear of eternal consequences, they were far more likely to disrupt society.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Bayle (1647–1706) observed that in his day that "prudent persons will always maintain an appearance of [religion]" and he believed that even atheists could hold concepts of honor and go beyond their own self-interest to create and interact in society.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Locke said that if there were no God and no divine law, the result would be moral anarchy: every individual "could have no law but his own will, no end but himself. He would be a god to himself, and the satisfaction of his own will the sole measure and end of all his actions".<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Separation_of_church_and_state">Separation of church and state</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Enlightenment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Separation of church and state">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">Separation of church and state</a> and <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state_in_the_United_States" title="Separation of church and state in the United States">Separation of church and state in the United States</a></div>
<p>The "Radical Enlightenment"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsrael201111_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsrael201111-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsrael201019_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsrael201019-75">[75]</a></sup> promoted the concept of separating church and state,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsrael2010vii.E2.80.93viii_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsrael2010vii.E2.80.93viii-76">[76]</a></sup> an idea that is often credited to English philosopher <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> (1632–1704).<sup id="cite_ref-AFP_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AFP-77">[77]</a></sup> According to his principle of the <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a>, Locke said that the government lacked authority in the realm of individual conscience, as this was something rational people could not cede to the government for it or others to control. For Locke, this created a natural right in the liberty of conscience, which he said must therefore remain protected from any government authority.</p>
<p>These views on religious tolerance and the importance of individual conscience, along with the social contract, became particularly influential in the American colonies and the drafting of the United States Constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> called for a "wall of separation between church and state" at the federal level. He previously had supported successful efforts to disestablish the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> in Virginia<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> and authored the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Statute_for_Religious_Freedom" title="Virginia Statute for Religious Freedom">Virginia Statute for Religious Freedom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Jefferson's political ideals were greatly influenced by the writings of <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> whom he considered the three greatest men that ever lived.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="National_variations">National variations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Enlightenment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: National variations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe,_1700_-_1714.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Europe%2C_1700_-_1714.png/440px-Europe%2C_1700_-_1714.png" width="440" height="301" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Europe%2C_1700_-_1714.png/660px-Europe%2C_1700_-_1714.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Europe%2C_1700_-_1714.png/880px-Europe%2C_1700_-_1714.png 2x" data-file-width="1590" data-file-height="1086" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe,_1700_-_1714.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Europe at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Spanish_Succession" title="War of the Spanish Succession">War of the Spanish Succession</a>, 1700</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Enlightenment took hold in most European countries, often with a specific local emphasis. For example, in France it became associated with anti-government and anti-Church radicalism while in Germany it reached deep into the middle classes and where it expressed a spiritualistic and nationalistic tone without threatening governments or established churches.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Government responses varied widely. In France, the government was hostile and the <i>philosophes</i> fought against its censorship, sometimes being imprisoned or hounded into exile. The British government for the most part ignored the Enlightenment's leaders in England and Scotland, although it did give Isaac Newton a knighthood and a very lucrative government office.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="England">England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Enlightenment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Georgian_era#English_Enlightenment" title="Georgian era">Georgian era §&#160;English Enlightenment</a></div>
<p>The very existence of an English Enlightenment has been hotly debated by scholars. The majority of textbooks on British history make little or no mention of an English Enlightenment. Some surveys of the entire Enlightenment include England and others ignore it, but do include has enlightenment intellectuals such representative Englishmen as Newton, Locke and Jonathan Swift, although they do include coverage of such major intellectuals as Joseph Addison, Edward Gibbon, John Locke, Isaac Newton, Alexander Pope, Joshua Reynolds and Jonathan Swift.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Roy_Porter" title="Roy Porter">Roy Porter</a> argues that the reason for the neglect was the assumption that the movement was primarily French-inspired, that it was largely a-religious or anti-clerical and it stood in outspoken defiance to the established order.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Porter admits that after the 1720s England could claim few thinkers to equal Diderot, Voltaire or Rousseau. Indeed, its leading intellectuals such as <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gibbon" title="Edward Gibbon">Edward Gibbon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Burke" title="Edmund Burke">Edmund Burke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a> were all quite conservative and supported of the standing order. Porter says the reason was that Enlightenment had come early to England and had succeeded so that the culture had accepted political liberalism, philosophical empiricism and religious toleration of the sort that intellectuals on the continent had to fight for against powerful odds. Furthermore, England rejected the collectivism of the continent and emphasized the improvement of individuals as the main goal of enlightenment<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AdamSmith.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/AdamSmith.jpg/170px-AdamSmith.jpg" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/AdamSmith.jpg/255px-AdamSmith.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/AdamSmith.jpg/340px-AdamSmith.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1456" data-file-height="2173" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AdamSmith.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
One leader of the Scottish Enlightenment was <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a>, the father of modern economic science</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scotland">Scotland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Enlightenment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Scotland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Enlightenment" title="Scottish Enlightenment">Scottish Enlightenment</a>, Scotland's major cities created an intellectual infrastructure of mutually supporting institutions such as universities, reading societies, libraries, periodicals, museums and masonic lodges.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsrael2011248.E2.80.9349_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsrael2011248.E2.80.9349-88">[88]</a></sup> The Scottish network was "predominantly liberal Calvinist, Newtonian, and 'design' oriented in character which played a major role in the further development of the transatlantic Enlightenment".<sup id="cite_ref-HermanTwo_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HermanTwo-89">[89]</a></sup> In France, <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> said that "we look to Scotland for all our ideas of civilization".<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The focus of the Scottish Enlightenment ranged from intellectual and economic matters to the specifically scientific as in the work of <a href="/wiki/William_Cullen" title="William Cullen">William Cullen</a>, physician and chemist; <a href="/wiki/James_Anderson_of_Hermiston" title="James Anderson of Hermiston">James Anderson</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Agronomist" class="mw-redirect" title="Agronomist">agronomist</a>; <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Black" title="Joseph Black">Joseph Black</a>, physicist and chemist; and <a href="/wiki/James_Hutton" title="James Hutton">James Hutton</a>, the first modern geologist.<sup id="cite_ref-Denby_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denby-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Repcheck_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Repcheck-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="American_colonies">American colonies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Enlightenment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: American colonies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/American_Enlightenment" title="American Enlightenment">American Enlightenment</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Declaration_of_Independence_(1819),_by_John_Trumbull.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg/220px-Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg/330px-Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg/440px-Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2976" data-file-height="1969" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Declaration_of_Independence_(1819),_by_John_Trumbull.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/John_Trumbull" title="John Trumbull">John Trumbull</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Trumbull%27s_Declaration_of_Independence" class="mw-redirect" title="Trumbull's Declaration of Independence"><i>Declaration of Independence</i></a> shows the drafting committee presenting its work to the Congress</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Several Americans, especially <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, played a major role in bringing Enlightenment ideas to the New World and in influencing British and French thinkers.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Franklin was influential for his political activism and for his advances in physics.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> The cultural exchange during the Age of Enlightenment ran in both directions across the Atlantic. Thinkers such as Paine, Locke and Rousseau all take Native American cultural practices as examples of natural freedom.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The Americans closely followed English and Scottish political ideas, as well as some French thinkers such as <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> As deists, they were influenced by ideas of John Toland (1670–1722) and Matthew Tindal (1656–1733). During the Enlightenment there was a great emphasis upon <a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Liberalism in the United States">liberty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">republicanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion_in_the_United_States" title="Freedom of religion in the United States">religious tolerance</a>. There was no respect for monarchy or inherited political power. Deists reconciled science and religion by rejecting prophecies, miracles and Biblical theology. Leading deists included <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Reason" title="The Age of Reason">The Age of Reason</a></i> and by Thomas Jefferson in his short <i><a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Bible" title="Jefferson Bible">Jefferson Bible</a></i> – from which all supernatural aspects were removed.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="German_states">German states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Enlightenment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: German states">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Germany#Enlightenment" title="History of Germany">History of Germany §&#160;Enlightenment</a></div>
<p>Prussia took the lead among the German states in sponsoring the political reforms that Enlightenment thinkers urged absolute rulers to adopt. There were important movements as well in the smaller states of Bavaria, Saxony, Hanover and the Palatinate. In each case, Enlightenment values became accepted and led to significant political and administrative reforms that laid the groundwork for the creation of modern states.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The princes of Saxony, for example, carried out an impressive series of fundamental fiscal, administrative, judicial, educational, cultural and general economic reforms. The reforms were aided by the country's strong urban structure and influential commercial groups and modernized pre-1789 Saxony along the lines of classic Enlightenment principles.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oer-Weimarer_Musenhof.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Oer-Weimarer_Musenhof.jpg/220px-Oer-Weimarer_Musenhof.jpg" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Oer-Weimarer_Musenhof.jpg/330px-Oer-Weimarer_Musenhof.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Oer-Weimarer_Musenhof.jpg/440px-Oer-Weimarer_Musenhof.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3168" data-file-height="2448" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oer-Weimarer_Musenhof.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Weimar’s Courtyard of the Muses</i>, a tribute to <a href="/wiki/The_Enlightenment" class="mw-redirect" title="The Enlightenment">The Enlightenment</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Classicism" title="Weimar Classicism">Weimar Classicism</a> depicting German poets <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Schiller" title="Friedrich Schiller">Schiller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Martin_Wieland" title="Christoph Martin Wieland">Wieland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_Herder" title="Johann Gottfried Herder">Herder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Goethe</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Before 1750, the German upper classes looked to France for intellectual, cultural and architectural leadership, as French was the language of high society. By the mid-18th century, the <i>Aufklärung</i> (<a href="/wiki/The_Enlightenment" class="mw-redirect" title="The Enlightenment">The Enlightenment</a>) had transformed German high culture in music, philosophy, science and literature. <a href="/wiki/Christian_Wolff_(philosopher)" title="Christian Wolff (philosopher)">Christian Wolff</a> (1679–1754) was the pioneer as a writer who expounded the Enlightenment to German readers and legitimized German as a philosophic language.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_von_Herder" class="mw-redirect" title="Johann Gottfried von Herder">Johann Gottfried von Herder</a> (1744–1803) broke new ground in philosophy and poetry, as a leader of the <a href="/wiki/Sturm_und_Drang" title="Sturm und Drang">Sturm und Drang</a> movement of proto-Romanticism. <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Classicism" title="Weimar Classicism">Weimar Classicism</a> (<i>Weimarer Klassik</i>) was a cultural and literary movement based in Weimar that sought to establish a new humanism by synthesizing Romantic, classical and Enlightenment ideas. The movement (from 1772 until 1805) involved Herder as well as polymath <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a> (1749–1832) and <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Schiller" title="Friedrich Schiller">Friedrich Schiller</a> (1759–1805), a poet and historian. Herder argued that every folk had its own particular identity, which was expressed in its language and culture. This legitimized the promotion of German language and culture and helped shape the development of German nationalism. Schiller's plays expressed the restless spirit of his generation, depicting the hero's struggle against social pressures and the force of destiny.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>German music, sponsored by the upper classes, came of age under composers <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Johann Sebastian Bach</a> (1685–1750), <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Haydn" title="Joseph Haydn">Joseph Haydn</a> (1732–1809) and <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart</a> (1756–1791).<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>In remote <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6nigsberg" title="Königsberg">Königsberg</a>, philosopher <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a> (1724–1804) tried to reconcile rationalism and religious belief, individual freedom and political authority. Kant's work contained basic tensions that would continue to shape German thought – and indeed all of European philosophy – well into the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>The German Enlightenment won the support of princes, aristocrats and the middle classes and it permanently reshaped the culture.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> However, there was a conservatism among the elites that warned against going too far.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1780s, Lutheran ministers <a href="/wiki/Johann_Heinrich_Schulz" title="Johann Heinrich Schulz">Johann Heinrich Schulz</a> and Karl Wilhelm Brumbey got in trouble with their preaching as they were attacked and ridiculed by Immanuel Kant, <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Abraham_Teller" title="Wilhelm Abraham Teller">Wilhelm Abraham Teller</a> and others. In 1788, Prussia issued an "Edict on Religion" that forbade preaching any sermon that undermined popular belief in the Holy Trinity and the Bible. The goal was to avoid skepticism, deism and theological disputes that might impinge on domestic tranquility. Men who doubted the value of Enlightenment favoured the measure, but so too did many supporters. German universities had created a closed elite that could debate controversial issues among themselves, but spreading them to the public was seen as too risky. This intellectual elite was favoured by the state, but that might be reversed if the process of the Enlightenment proved politically or socially destabilizing.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Italy">Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Enlightenment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Italy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Enlightenment played a distinctive, if small, role in the history of Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Although most of Italy was controlled by conservative Habsburgs or the pope, Tuscany had some opportunities for reform. <a href="/wiki/Leopold_II,_Grand_Duke_of_Tuscany" title="Leopold II, Grand Duke of Tuscany">Leopold II of Tuscany</a> abolished the death penalty in Tuscany and reduced censorship. From Naples, <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Genovesi" title="Antonio Genovesi">Antonio Genovesi</a> (1713-69) influenced a generation of southern Italian intellectuals and university students. His textbook "Diceosina, o Sia della Filosofia del Giusto e dell'Onesto" (1766) was a controversial attempt to mediate between the history of moral philosophy on the one hand and the specific problems encountered by 18th-century commercial society on the other. It contained the greater part of Genovesi's political, philosophical and economic thought – guidebook for Neapolitan economic and social development.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Science flourished as <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">Alessandro Volta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Galvani" title="Luigi Galvani">Luigi Galvani</a> made break-through discoveries in electricity. <a href="/wiki/Pietro_Verri" title="Pietro Verri">Pietro Verri</a> was a leading economist in Lombardy. Historian <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Schumpeter" title="Joseph Schumpeter">Joseph Schumpeter</a> states he was "the most important pre-Smithian authority on Cheapness-and-Plenty".<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The most influential scholar on the Italian Enlightenment has been <a href="/wiki/Franco_Venturi" title="Franco Venturi">Franco Venturi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Enlightenment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Russia, the government began to actively encourage the proliferation of arts and sciences in the mid-18th century. This era produced the first Russian university, library, theatre, public museum and independent press. Like other <a href="/wiki/Enlightened_absolutism" title="Enlightened absolutism">enlightened despots</a>, Catherine the Great played a key role in fostering the arts, sciences and education. She used her own interpretation of Enlightenment ideals, assisted by notable international experts such as Voltaire (by correspondence) and in residence world class scientists such as <a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Simon_Pallas" title="Peter Simon Pallas">Peter Simon Pallas</a>. The national Enlightenment differed from its Western European counterpart in that it promoted further <a href="/wiki/Modernization" class="mw-redirect" title="Modernization">modernization</a> of all aspects of Russian life and was concerned with attacking the institution of <a href="/wiki/Serfdom_in_Russia" title="Serfdom in Russia">serfdom in Russia</a>. The Russian enlightenment centered on the individual instead of societal enlightenment and encouraged the living of an enlightened life.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsrael2011609.E2.80.93632_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsrael2011609.E2.80.93632-114">[114]</a></sup> A powerful element was <i>prosveshchenie</i> which combined religious piety, erudition and commitment to the spread of learning.
